## Voice Reflection System (2026-01-15)

### Overview

**Voice Reflections** are derivative, attribution-safe paraphrased voice fragments generated from existing teachings. They are **NOT quotes** - they are "voice-consistent reflective paraphrases" that can be used when quote pools are sparse.

**Key Principle:** Recognition-only, clearly marked as derived, never used as authority.

### Implementation

**File:** tools/generate_voice_reflections.py

**Features:**
- Generates reflections from existing teachings (2-3 teachings per reflection)
- Max 30 reflections per teacher
- Hard limit: < 200 characters
- Recognition-only validation (no cures, promises, commands)
- Automatic generation during knowledge base analysis

**Governance Rules:**
- Max 1 per book (hard limit)
- Only used when quote pool is low (< 5 quotes)
- Always prefaced with "In the spirit of…" or "A reflection in the voice of…"
- Tracked separately from quotes

**Integration:**
- Automatically generated by analyze_teacher_knowledge_base.py
- Tracked separately in simulation system
- Available in omega_policy.allow_voice_reflections

**Output:**
- teachers/{teacher_id}/knowledge_base/voice_reflections.json
- Schema includes: reflection_id, source_teaching_ids, text, tone, intensity, use_context

**Test Results:**
- ✅ Generated 30 voice reflections for Master Sha
- ✅ Recognition safety validation working
- ✅ Integration with knowledge base analyzer working

**Documentation:**
- docs/VOICE_REFLECTION_SYSTEM.md - Complete system documentation

---

### Phase 5: Automation & Scale  
- **Batch generation**: 100s-1000s of books with different configurations
- **QA automation**: Automated validation of all build parameters
- **Analytics-ready manifests**: Complete traceability for business intelligence

### Phase 6: Content Velocity
- **New modules**: Plug-and-play expansion of module library
- **New structure families**: Additional structural templates
- **New personas**: Expanded audience targeting

The architecture supports all future expansion without requiring changes to existing books or breaking existing workflows.

---

## Summary: System Status & Usage

### When to Use Each System

**Phoenix Raising:**
- Generating books for any persona/topic combination
- Creating books in specific format structures (7-day reset, morning ritual, etc.)
- Batch generation of 100+ books
- Platform-optimized audiobook production
- When you need generic content that works for all personas/topics

**Phoenix Omega Template System:**
- Legacy template-based books
- When you need deterministic variable substitution
- Fixed 12-chapter structure
- No story engine needed

**Phoenix Omega V3:**
- Short-form books (~8-12k words)
- Fast, deterministic assembly
- When you need governed story injection
- Production-ready, frozen architecture

**Phoenix Omega V4:**
- Long-form books (40-80k+ words)
- Section-first planning with mandatory variants
- When you need high-quality, platform-safe books
- When you need full exercise text with intro and ah_ha
- Production-ready with simulation system

### Key Architectural Principles (V3 & V4)

1. **LLM = Planner (offline, once)** — LLM generates plan, never assembles
2. **System = Assembler (deterministic, no decisions)** — Python only looks up and stitches
3. **Frozen plan is the single source of truth** — Plan YAML contains all decisions
4. **Hard-fail validation** — Validator enforces policies before assembly
5. **Assembler is lookup-only** — No runtime creativity or fallbacks
6. **No runtime creativity** — All content from libraries, no generation
7. **Auditability > cleverness** — Every decision traceable to plan

### Story Engine Clarification

**CORRECT:**
- ✅ Phoenix Raising uses the story engine (StoryLoader + NarrativeFlow)
- ✅ Phoenix Omega V3 uses the story engine (governed via Story Slot Policy)
- ✅ Phoenix Omega V4 uses the story engine (governed, persona-aware 4-tier selection)

**INCORRECT (from old chat):**
- ❌ "Phoenix Omega does not use the story engine" — This is only true for the Template System, NOT for V3 or V4

### Exercise Engine Clarification

**Phoenix Raising:**
- Uses NarrativeFlow for exercise selection
- Exercises from phoenix_raising_somatic_37_final.json (Phoenix somatic bank)

**Phoenix Omega Template System:**
- Exercises via variable placeholders ({exercise_1.name}, etc.)
- Resolved from YAML values files

**Phoenix Omega V3:**
- Uses practice adapter (brief/full mode)
- Exercises from phoenix_v3/libraries/exercises/

**Phoenix Omega V4:**
- Full exercise text with intro_hook, main content, and ah_ha
- Sources: exercises_ab_tady_37_PRODUCTION_READY.json + 9 library_34.json files
- Cumulative ah_ha variants (180w, 340w, 480w)

Yes — you can make Phoenix Raising’s generic CSV bank serve Omega V4, but it’s not a simple “swap slots and go.” It’s a translation layer problem: V4 assembles section-variants from a frozen plan, while Phoenix Raising renders a continuous prose container from a CSV + slot hydration, then injects stories/exercises via beat-map assembly.

SYSTEMS_DOCUMENTATION_2026-01-07
That said, there are two viable ways to do it.

Option A (recommended): Convert Phoenix Raising CSV into a V4 Section Library
Goal: Treat the generic CSV as a source for V4’s section registry, not as the runtime renderer.
How it works
Split the Phoenix Raising CSV into V4 section types Map your CSV “SECTION_01..SECTION_10” style blocks into V4’s section taxonomy (HOOK / TEACHING / SCENE / REFLECTION / INTEGRATION / EXERCISE, etc.).
Create ≥3 variants per section (≥5 for high-risk sections) V4 requires mandatory variants; Phoenix Raising is “10 styles,” which can become variants, but you still need to ensure coverage rules align.  SYSTEMS_DOCUMENTATION_2026-01-07 
Slot-bridge / token normalization
Strip or replace Phoenix Raising-only tokens.
Replace with V4’s canonical slot keys (whatever your V4 plan expects).
Add a strict validator: “no unresolved {...} tokens in final output” (you already treat that as a production-critical rule in Raising).  SYSTEMS_DOCUMENTATION_2026-01-07 
Register into phoenix_v4/libraries/section_loader.py So V4’s assembler can select deterministically by plan.
Why this is best
Keeps V4’s “LLM = planner, Python = lookup-only assembler” principle intact.  SYSTEMS_DOCUMENTATION_2026-01-07 
Avoids teaching V4 how to interpret Raising’s CSV runtime logic.
Produces clean “content libraries” that V4 can simulate and validate at scale.

Option B (fastest “get something working”): Use Phoenix Raising CSV as a V4 fallback prose-container
Goal: In V4, for certain “container” sections, render a block from the Raising CSV and drop it into V4 output.
How it works
Add a V4 “container adapter” that:
selects a Raising section deterministically (seed + chapter + section_type)
hydrates with a V4 slot map
outputs text as a “section variant”
Why it’s riskier
You’re effectively making V4 partially depend on Raising’s rendering assumptions.
It can violate the “lookup-only assembler” vibe unless you freeze all decisions into plan YAML.
Harder to guarantee V4’s variant coverage rules without doing Option A anyway.

Cleanliness (hard gates)
unresolved_vars_count (should be 0)

3) Failure bucketing (this is the “learn” part)
Don’t just “fail”; classify.
Bucket categories (practical)
LOC_ARTIFACT
Examples: “in .”, “at ,”, “went to .”
PUNCT_DANGLING
“ ,” “ .” “( )” “—” isolated
REPETITION_NGRAM
6+gram repeats across books beyond threshold
PARA_DUPLICATE
Exact paragraph collision too often
OPENING_COLLISION
Same first 10 words too frequent
EXERCISE_OVERDENSITY
Too many long practices / too instructional
STORY_OVERFREQUENCY
Same story appears in too many outputs
Each bucket should output:
count
examples (seed + chapter + excerpt)
fix recommendation (which gate/policy/variant set)
4) The key enhancement you already need: “Location Repair Gate”
Right now you’re stripping {location.*} and leaving grammar scars. That’s exactly what 10k sims will surface.
Implement a deterministic Gate 5: Surface Repair, with targeted, testable rewrites:

A. Phrase-level deletions (safe)
r"\b(in|at|on)\s*[.,]" -> ""
r"\b(to|from)\s*[.,]" -> ""
r"\s+,([.!?])" -> r"\1"
r"\s+\.+" -> "."
r"\(\s*\)" -> ""
B. Neutral fallbacks (better than deletion in some slots)
If token removed in a SCENE, replace with neutral anchor:
{location.digital_space} → “online” / “on your phone”
{location.social_gathering} → “a get-together” / “a place with people”
Important: do this in the hydrator before removal, or do a post-pass that detects “in .” and inserts fallback based on section_type=SCENE.
Simulation will tell you which strategy yields fewer artifacts.

5) Corpus-wide spam-risk scoring
After 10k outputs, compute:
A. Collision rates
% books sharing any paragraph hash
% books sharing any 6-gram above K occurrences
% books sharing identical section openings
B. “Spam score” heuristic (simple, effective)
Weighted score per book:
+5 if any LOC_ARTIFACT
+3 if any repeated paragraph seen >N times
+2 if 6-gram collision count high
+1 if opening collisions high
Then rank:
worst 100 books (investigate)
worst 10 sections (add variants)
6) What to fix based on results (typical outcomes)
If LOC_ARTIFACT dominates (likely)
✅ Add Gate 5 + fallback strategy
✅ Add section-level rule: scenes must include a non-token anchor (“on your phone”) so removal still reads fine
If paragraph collisions dominate
✅ Increase variants in high-collision sections (often REFLECTION/INTEGRATION)
✅ Enforce “minimum variant count” for ALL sections, not just HOOK/SCENE
If openings collide
✅ Add “first sentence diversity” validator across variant libraries
If stories over-repeat
✅ Add story-frequency cap per simulation batch
✅ Expand story bank tiers, or broaden adjacent-topic pool
7) CI integration (so this doesn’t regress)
Add 3 layers:
A. PR quick gate (fast)
50 seeds
must be 0 hard-gate fails
LOC_ARTIFACT must be 0

1,000 seeds

10,000 seeds

yes all
Got it — here are (1) hard numeric safety thresholds, (2) exactly what sections to expand, and (3) a minimal-effort variant expansion playbook that your content team + dev can run like a factory.
1) Hard numeric thresholds for “not spam” safety (practical, testable)
These aren’t “Google/Apple official” (they don’t publish exact numbers). These are engineering thresholds that correlate strongly with “this won’t look mass-duplicated” when you do large-scale generation.
A. Book-level similarity gates (10,000-run simulation)
Gate A1 — Duplicate book rate
Target: 0 exact duplicates out of 10,000
Fail: ≥ 1 exact duplicate
Gate A2 — Near-duplicate rate (fingerprint similarity)
Define a simple similarity score per pair:
Compare paragraph-hash sets (or shingled paragraph hashes)
Flag a “near-duplicate” if ≥ 80% paragraphs match
Target: ≤ 0.05% of books have a near-duplicate (≤ 5 / 10,000)
Warn: 0.05%–0.2%
Fail: > 0.2%
Gate A3 — Chapter-level identicalness
Target: For each chapter number (1..12), the % of runs that produce the exact same chapter text should be:
HOOK/SCENE/INTEGRATION sections present: ≤ 2%
Otherwise (low-variance chapters): ≤ 5%
Fail: any chapter ≥ 10%
Why: even if whole books aren’t identical, a marketplace can still see “mass-reused chapter text”.
B. Component collision gates (what your current report is hinting at)
You should track collisions by section_id and by section_type.
Gate B1 — Unique paragraph hashes
Measure: unique_paragraph_hashes / total_unique_paragraphs_possible
In practice, just use a per-run aggregate:

Target: ≥ 2,000 unique paragraph hashes across 10k runs (for a 12-chapter book with your structure)
Warn: 1,000–2,000
Fail: < 1,000
(Your current 1k-run shows 234 unique paragraph hashes, which is fine for early-stage + limited variants, but it won’t scale safely unless you expand the right sections.)
Gate B2 — “Hot paragraph” dominance
Count the most common paragraph hash frequency.

Target: top paragraph appears in ≤ 0.5% of books (≤ 50 / 10,000)
Warn: 0.5%–1%
Fail: > 1%
Gate B3 — 6-gram reuse
You already track “6-gram collisions above 10 occurrences.” Tighten it:
Target: #(6-grams occurring > 50 times) = 0
Warn: 1–20
Fail: > 20
This prevents repeated “signature phrases” that make outputs look templated.
C. Story diversity gates (must fix instrumentation first)
Once story IDs are tracked:
Gate C1 — Unique story IDs

Target: ≥ 250 unique story IDs used across 10k runs (assuming your bank is large)
Warn: 100–250
Fail: < 100
Gate C2 — Hot story dominance
Target: top story used ≤ 0.5% of books
Fail: > 1%
2) Exactly which sections to expand (highest ROI)
You do not need to expand everything equally. Expand the sections that create most collisions.
Priority order (do these first)
Tier 1: Must expand (highest platform risk)
INTEGRATION (book framing, “what this is / isn’t”, closing invitation)
REFLECTION / TEACHING (pattern explanation, core reframe, thesis preview)
HOOK (already has variants — keep strong)
Why: These are the sections that tend to repeat across books and scream “templated self-help”.
Tier 2: Expand next (good ROI, lower risk)
SCENE (already good; expand by location + persona context richness)
BRIDGES / TRANSITIONS (tiny but powerful collision reducers)
Tier 3: Optional / later
Micro-copy blocks (one-liners, “pause” sentences, rhetorical questions)
3) Variant Expansion Playbook (minimal effort, maximal uniqueness)
This is how your content team should write variants so you get real uniqueness without ballooning workload.
Step 0 — Dev produces a “Collision Heatmap”
From 1k or 10k runs, output:
Top 25 section_ids by:
paragraph reuse
sentence reuse
6-gram hotspots
Top repeated phrases (6–10 grams)
“First sentence” collisions per section_id
This tells content exactly what to rewrite first.
Step 1 — Upgrade variant policy (what content must deliver)
Required variant counts (per section type):
HOOK: 7 variants
SCENE: 7 variants
REFLECTION / TEACHING: 5 variants (currently your weak point)
INTEGRATION: 7 variants (highest risk)
BRIDGE / TRANSITION: 9 micro-variants (short)
If your workload is tight:
Do INTEGRATION + REFLECTION first. That alone usually collapses collision rates.
Step 2 — Variant Families (F1–F5) must apply to every section, not just scenes/hooks
Right now you’re treating F1–F5 as mainly HOOK/SCENE. For platform safety, do it for REFLECTION/INTEGRATION too.
Use this mapping:

F1 Sensory/Body: “Here’s what it feels like…”
F2 Dialogue/Inner voice: “You tell yourself…”
F3 Context/Mechanism: “Here’s the loop…”
F4 Imagery/Metaphor: “It’s like…”
F5 Consequence/Cost: “And the cost is…”
Rule: same meaning, different cognitive entry point.
Step 3 — “Anchor + Swap” method (fast writing, big uniqueness)
Instead of rewriting whole sections from scratch:
Each section variant is built from:

Anchor sentences (2–3 sentences that preserve the chapter’s truth)
Swap blocks (3–6 sentences that change)
Example for REFLECTION:
Keep 2 anchor lines: the core reframe
Swap: mechanism explanation, examples, metaphors, cadence
This yields fast variants without losing voice.
Step 4 — Micro-variants (the cheapest collision killer)
For each chapter, create a micro-variant bank:
Openers (10)
Closers (10)
Bridge lines (15)
Rhetorical questions (15)
Then each REFLECTION / INTEGRATION section includes:
{micro.opener}
{micro.bridge}
{micro.closer}
These are short, but they shatter paragraph hashing collisions.
If you don’t want variables: treat them as append-only inserts chosen deterministically.

Step 5 — “Do not repeat” rules (content team checklist)
Hard rules that reduce platform fingerprints:
No repeated first 12 words across variants in the same section_id
No repeated last sentence across variants in the same section_id
Banlist any phrase that appears too often (generated by dev report)
Avoid stable “template tells” like:
“Here’s the thing—”
“Let me be clear—”
“What nobody tells you—”
(Use them, but rotate aggressively.)

Right now Tier 1 is absolute. That’s the real issue.
New rule: Tier Saturation Softening
Conceptually:
“Perfect matches are preferred — but not endlessly.”
Deterministic softening logic
Add a story usage counter keyed by (persona, topic, layer) during simulation:
if story_usage_count[story_id] >= SATURATION_THRESHOLD:
    reduce_weight(story_id)
Suggested values:
SATURATION_THRESHOLD = 50 (per 10k run)
After threshold:
Tier 1 weight drops from 2.0 → 1.0
Tier 2 remains at 1.5
Tier 3 remains at 1.0
Result:
Early runs still favor Tier 1
Later runs naturally spread across Tier 2 / Tier 3
Still deterministic (depends on run index + seed)
This mimics editorial diversity, not randomness.

You do not need 100 totally new stories.

story_family_id: genz_nyc_anxiety_classroom
variants:
  - id: genz_nyc_anxiety_classroom_v1
  - id: genz_nyc_anxiety_classroom_v2
  - id: genz_nyc_anxiety_classroom_v3
Rules:
Same emotional truth
Different:
opening sentence
sensory details
internal dialogue
resolution beat
The injector treats each variant as a unique story ID.
📌 This is the highest ROI fix.
3–5 variants per family is enough.

Below is a clean, system-doc–accurate description of the story engine, focusing on what writes/selects stories, which files are involved, and what each function is responsible for. Phoenix Story Engine — How Stories Are Written, Selected, and Injected
Important framing: The story engine does not “write” stories at runtime. Stories are pre-authored or pre-generated, stored in libraries, and deterministically selected and injected during assembly.
This distinction is core to the architecture.

1. What the Story Engine Actually Does
The story engine is responsible for:
Loading pre-existing story assets
Filtering them by persona, topic, tier, and section
Selecting one eligible story per story slot
Enforcing governance rules (no repetition, spacing, tier priority)
Injecting the chosen story into the book during assembly
Reporting usage to simulation / analytics
It does not:
Generate prose during assembly
Rewrite stories
Randomize choices at runtime
This applies to Phoenix Raising, Phoenix Omega V3, and Phoenix Omega V4 (❌ not used by the legacy Template System)

SYSTEMS_DOCUMENTATION_2026-01-07

2. High-Level Architecture


Story Libraries (JSON)
        ↓
StoryLoader
        ↓
NarrativeFlow / Story Slot Policy
        ↓
Story Validator
        ↓
Story Thread Tracker
        ↓
Assembler injects story text


3. Story Sources (Where Stories Come From)
Stories live in JSON libraries, grouped by type and tier.
Types of stories supported
From the system doc:
Real teacher stories (highest priority)
Reflective Case Stories (generated, illustrative, second-order)
Generic stories (lowest tier fallback)
Reflective Case Stories are generated offline, not during assembly, by:


tools/generate_reflective_case_stories.py

and stored per teacher at:

persona tags
topic tags
tier (T1 / T2 / T3)
section compatibility

No selection logic

No mutation

Receive:
plan-defined story slots
candidate story pool from StoryLoader

Tier 1: persona + topic exact
Tier 2: persona or topic match
Tier 3: generic fallback
Enforce:
one story per slot
no adjacent reuse
priority ordering
Important: Selection is deterministic, driven by:
book_seed
chapter
slot_id
No randomness is allowed.

ChatGPT said:
You’re right to call this out — somatic_generic_with_10_styles.csv is a prose template, not stories.
Here’s the clear, correct answer to where the story code actually lives in Phoenix Raising.

Short answer
Phoenix Raising stories are NOT in the CSV. They are loaded, selected, and injected by Python story engine files, then inserted into the CSV at assembly time.

Where the story .py files are (Phoenix Raising)
1. Story loading
Purpose: Read prewritten stories from disk into memory.
story_loader.py
Loads reusable story JSONs
Indexes by persona, topic, tier, tags
No selection logic, no writing
This is where “the stories” first enter the system.

2. Story selection (runtime)
Purpose: Decide which story goes into which slot.
narrative_flow.py
Chooses stories for each story slot
Applies tier weighting (T1/T2/T3)
Enforces no-adjacent-reuse rules
Deterministic (seed + slot)
Important: narrative_flow.py does not write stories. It only selects from what story_loader.py provides.

3. Story validation / governance
Purpose: Ensure platform-safe stories.
story_validator.py
Recognition-only checks
No cures / promises
Third-person enforcement (for reflective case stories)
Hard-fail on violations

4. Story reuse tracking
Purpose: Prevent repetition + support analytics.
story_thread_tracker.py
Tracks which story IDs were used
Prevents reuse within a book
Feeds simulation metrics

5. Story writing (offline, NOT runtime)
This is separate from Phoenix Raising runtime.
Stories are written earlier by offline generator scripts, then stored as JSON for the engine to load:
Examples (from your repo reality):
story_generator.py
tasks.py
generate_reusable_stories.py
save_story_helper.py
background writers (write_all_stories_*.py)
reflective case writer (generate_reflective_case_stories.py)
These produce JSONs like:


story_bank/reusable/*.json
teachers/*/knowledge_base/reflective_case_stories.json

Phoenix Raising only consumes these files.

How it all fits together (Phoenix Raising)


Offline story writers (.py)
        ↓
Reusable story JSONs on disk
        ↓
story_loader.py
        ↓
narrative_flow.py
        ↓
CSV template (somatic_generic_with_10_styles.csv)
        ↓
Final book text

So:
The CSV = structure + prose scaffolding
The stories = JSON assets written earlier
The story engine = Python code that loads + selects + injects

Prewrite stories
Let V4 select + inject only
You’ve identified the separation correctly.

Persona/topic: Copy from plan header (plan.persona_id, plan.topic_id). Include language and location if present.
Slot identity: Deterministic slot_id such as ch{chapter:02d}_{section_id}_{layer} so the prebuilder/writer can hash to stable story_ids.
Need count: Usually 1 per slot; if a section lists multiple story_plan entries, emit one requirement per entry (explicit IDs keep 1; auto_select keeps 1).
Use past plans? Optional only for metrics/coverage suggestions, never to change current requirements. You can load prior coverage reports to recommend higher min_unique_story_ids, but the extractor itself should be pure: current plan + registry → requirements.
So: the extractor is deterministic and schema-driven; counts and types come from registry + fixed coverage policy (5+ uniques per slot), not from past plans or runtime availability.

💡 Peer & Social Influence in Personal Change
Social relationships actively shape behavior, habits, and well-being. People tend to adopt behaviors, emotional patterns, and values seen in those close to them — friends, peers, family, coworkers, etc. This is a well-recognized social effect in psychology:

Workplace friendships shift communication, trust, and personal discourse beyond task roles, affecting how people express and cope with stress. 
Books addressing work stress and resilience include coping strategies that involve strengthening social bonds at work (e.g., between managers and employees).

Reciprocal determinism suggests behavior is shaped by a dynamic loop between personal traits, behavior, and environment — including social context and feedback from others. 
Social psychology also covers how significant others influence self-concept and behavior.

📘 Story Spec: Peer/Social Reaction Guideline (Validator Rule)
Purpose: Ensure stories for Gen Z and Gen Alpha personas reflect the social context that actually shapes behavior, not just internal thoughts — because peer influence is a documented force in how young people interpret experiences and change their behaviors.

📗 Rule (Formal)
For any story where the persona is Gen Z or Gen Alpha and the topic involves identity, behavior change, habits, confidence, self-improvement, or social emotion:
The story must include at least one peer/social interaction or reaction that meaningfully affects the protagonist’s internal experience, choice point, or emotional transformation.
Peer/social interaction means one of the following appears in the narrative:
Direct behavior influence A scene where a friend, coworker, classmate, peer group, or other social contact influences the protagonist’s feelings, choices, or reflections.
Social feedback A reaction (positive or negative) from someone in the protagonist’s social network that alters the narrative direction or deepens the emotional impact.
Normative contrast A comparison or contrast between protagonist’s emerging behavior and the expectations or norms of their peers/community.
Why: Social influence alters beliefs, behaviors, and norms — especially among young people who rely on peer networks for validation and learning.

📊 Validator Logic (Enforceable)
Input: Story text + persona tag + topic tag
Conditions:
If persona ∈ {Gen Z, Gen Alpha} AND topic ∈ {identity, habits, confidence, belonging, social validation, self-improvement}
Then check:
Does the story contain peer reaction text (e.g., friend reaction, group comment, coworker feedback, social contrast)?
Output:
WARN if no peer interaction detected (light tier)
FAIL (strong validator) if:
Tier = L2 or L3 (mid/long stories should include deeper social context), AND
No peer reaction appears
Rationale: Short, micro-stories (L1) may only hint at social context — a warning suffices. Medium/long narratives (L2/L3) must include it to feel realistic and psychologically grounded.

📘 Example Checks (Pseudo-Regex/Heuristic)
Match phrases like:
“My friend said…”
“They laughed when I…”
“At work, my coworker noticed…”
“In class, others reacted…”
“They didn’t understand me until…”
These show social influence or feedback in the narrative.

🧠 Why This Matters (Evidence)
Gen Z seeks connection but may misread social cues, and social connections contribute to well-being. Peer context shapes how young adults interpret others’ friendliness and support. 
Social media and peer engagement influence personal values and motivation, often shaping how Gen Z sees growth and success through feedback and group norms. 
Peer influence is central in development, supported by decades of research on how peers shape behavior, attitudes, and conformity — especially in adolescence and young adulthood. 
This guideline makes sure your stories don’t just talk about internal change — they also reflect the social ecosystem that people live in, which makes them more authentic and psychologically resonant.

📌 JSON Schema (Peer/Social Reaction Validator)


{
  "$schema": "http://json-schema.org/draft-07/schema#",
  "title": "Story Peer/Social Reaction Rule",
  "description": "Ensure stories for Gen Z/Gen Alpha include at least one peer/social interaction that meaningfully affects the protagonist.",
  "type": "object",
  "properties": {
    "persona": {
      "type": "string",
      "enum": ["Gen Z", "Gen Alpha"]
    },
    "topic": {
      "type": "string"
    },
    "story_text": {
      "type": "string",
      "minLength": 1
    }
  },
  "required": ["persona", "story_text"],
  "allOf": [
    {
      "if": {
        "properties": {
          "persona": {
            "enum": ["Gen Z", "Gen Alpha"]
          }
        }
      },
      "then": {
        "properties": {
          "story_text": {
            "type": "string",
            "pattern": "(friend|peer|coworker|classmate|teammate|boss|social group|reaction|encouraged|discouraged)"
          }
        },
        "errorMessage": "Gen Z/Gen Alpha stories must include at least one peer/social reaction that influences the protagonist."
      }
    }
  ]
}

What it checks:
If persona is Gen Z or Gen Alpha,
Then story_text must contain at least one indicator of a peer/social reaction (e.g., friend reaction, group response, coworker interaction, encouragement, social feedback). This enforces social influence inclusion.

📌 Example Test Cases
✅ Test Case: Valid Gen Z Story (pass)


{
  "persona": "Gen Z",
  "topic": "confidence",
  "story_text": "When Alex shared his idea in class, his classmates nodded and encouraged him, which boosted his confidence and helped him commit."
}

Why pass: Mentions classmates encouraging him — a peer reaction affecting experience. This reflects that peers influence behavior and confidence.

❌ Test Case: Missing Social Context (fail)

  "topic": "confidence",
  "story_text": "Alex decided to work on his confidence alone, focusing on self-talk and journaling every morning."

🚫 Non-Target Persona (no social rule enforced)

  "topic": "career change",
  "story_text": "She realized that her career was holding her back and started taking night classes."
}

Why no enforcement: Persona not Gen Z/Gen Alpha → standard rules apply, not this social requirement.

Peer relationships shape attitudes and behavior in adolescence and beyond — a core idea in developmental research. 
Gen Z specifically relies more on peer feedback and reviews for decisions, including health and community validation. 
Peer influence can be positive or adaptive, helping individuals align with supportive norms or behaviors. 
Validating peer reactions ensures your stories honor how social influence actually works in real human behavior.

🧠 Purpose
Ensure that stories generated for Gen Z and Gen Alpha personas meaningfully reflect social influence from peers, friends, coworkers, or similar relations, because peer influence is a well-documented psychological factor in behavior change and identity formation for young people.

🧾 Story Spec Rule (Formal)
When to apply:
Persona is Gen Z or Gen Alpha
Story topic relates to identity, behavior change, confidence, motivation, social belonging, habits, or self-improvement
Required narrative element: Story must include at least one instance of social influence that affects the protagonist’s feelings, choices, or emotional transformation — for example:
A peer’s encouragement or discouragement
A reaction from a friend or classmate
A boss/coworker comment that affects motivation
A social group feedback moment that shifts perspective
This reflects peer influence mechanisms in developmental psychology, where the presence and actions of peers affect attitudes and decisions.

If:
persona ∈ {“Gen Z”, “Gen Alpha”} AND
topic is in a list of socially relevant areas (identity, motivation, behavior, belonging, stress response, etc.)

The story text must contain at least one indicator of social influence/context.

coworker
boss
team

they reacted

L1 (short) — missing social influence = warning

Deeper stories should show richer social context.

peer_social_validator.py
"""
Peer/Social Reaction validator for Gen Z / Gen Alpha stories.

Purpose:
    Enforce the documented rule that stories for Gen Z / Gen Alpha personas
    include at least one socially influential reaction (peer/friend/coworker/etc.).

Behavior:
    - Applies only when persona is Gen Z or Gen Alpha AND topic is socially relevant.
    - L1 tier: warn if missing social context.
    - L2/L3 tiers: fail if missing social context.

Usage:
    from zencoder.rules.peer_social_validator import validate_story_social_context
    result = validate_story_social_context(persona="Gen Z",
                                           topic="confidence",
                                           tier="L2",
                                           story_text="...")
    result.status in {"pass", "warn", "fail"}
"""

import re
from dataclasses import dataclass
from typing import List, Literal, Optional

Tier = Literal["L1", "L2", "L3"]


# Topics where social influence is especially relevant
SOCIAL_TOPICS = {
    "identity",
    "behavior",
    "behavior_change",
    "habits",
    "confidence",
    "motivation",
    "belonging",
    "social_pressure",
    "self_improvement",
    "stress",
}

# Regex-friendly indicators of social reactions
SOCIAL_PATTERNS: List[str] = [
    r"friend",
    r"peer",
    r"classmate",
    r"coworker",
    r"teammate",
    r"boss",
    r"group",
    r"they reacted",
    r"they encouraged",
    r"they laughed",
    r"they noticed",
    r"my team",
    r"at work",
    r"at school",
]

SOCIAL_REGEX = re.compile("|".join(SOCIAL_PATTERNS), flags=re.IGNORECASE)


@dataclass
class ValidationResult:
    status: Literal["pass", "warn", "fail"]
    message: str


def _is_social_persona(persona: str) -> bool:
    return persona.strip().lower() in {"gen z", "gen alpha", "gen_z", "gen_alpha"}


def _is_social_topic(topic: str) -> bool:
    return topic.strip().lower() in SOCIAL_TOPICS


def _has_social_indicator(text: str) -> bool:
    return bool(SOCIAL_REGEX.search(text or ""))


def validate_story_social_context(
    *,
    persona: str,
    topic: str,
    tier: Tier,
    story_text: str,
) -> ValidationResult:
    """
    Validate that a story includes peer/social context when required.

    Returns:
        ValidationResult with status in {"pass", "warn", "fail"} and a message.
    """
    needs_social = _is_social_persona(persona) and _is_social_topic(topic)
    if not needs_social:
        return ValidationResult(
            status="pass",
            message="Social-context rule not applicable to this persona/topic.",
        )

    has_social = _has_social_indicator(story_text)

    if has_social:
        return ValidationResult(status="pass", message="Social context present.")

    if tier == "L1":
        return ValidationResult(
            status="warn",
            message="L1 story missing peer/social reaction; recommended to add.",
        )

    # L2/L3 must include social context
    return ValidationResult(
        status="fail",
        message="L2/L3 story missing required peer/social reaction.",
    )


# Convenience CLI hook (optional)
def main(argv: Optional[List[str]] = None) -> int:
    import argparse
    parser = argparse.ArgumentParser(description="Validate peer/social context in a story.")
    parser.add_argument("--persona", required=True, help="Persona name (e.g., 'Gen Z').")
    parser.add_argument("--topic", required=True, help="Topic name (e.g., 'confidence').")
    parser.add_argument("--tier", required=True, choices=["L1", "L2", "L3"], help="Story tier.")
    parser.add_argument("--file", help="Path to story text file; if omitted, read stdin.")
    args = parser.parse_args(argv)

    text: str
    if args.file:
        with open(args.file, "r", encoding="utf-8") as f:
            text = f.read()
    else:
        import sys
        text = sys.stdin.read()

    result = validate_story_social_context(
        persona=args.persona, topic=args.topic, tier=args.tier, story_text=text
    )
    print(f"{result.status.upper()}: {result.message}")
    return 0 if result.status in {"pass", "warn"} else 1


if __name__ == "__main__":
    raise SystemExit(main())
Created
test_peer_social_validator.py
import pytest

from zencoder.rules.peer_social_validator import validate_story_social_context


def test_pass_when_social_present():
    story = "Her friend encouraged her after the presentation."
    res = validate_story_social_context(
        persona="Gen Z", topic="confidence", tier="L2", story_text=story
    )
    assert res.status == "pass"


def test_fail_when_missing_social_in_l2():
    story = "She worked alone and eventually felt better."
    res = validate_story_social_context(
        persona="Gen Z", topic="confidence", tier="L2", story_text=story
    )
    assert res.status == "fail"


def test_warn_when_missing_social_in_l1():
    story = "He took a breath and started again."
    res = validate_story_social_context(
        persona="Gen Z", topic="confidence", tier="L1", story_text=story
    )
    assert res.status == "warn"


def test_not_applicable_other_persona():
    story = "He practiced alone."
    res = validate_story_social_context(
        persona="Baby Boomer", topic="confidence", tier="L2", story_text=story
    )
    assert res.status == "pass"


def test_not_applicable_other_topic():
    story = "He practiced alone."
    res = validate_story_social_context(
        persona="Gen Z", topic="finance", tier="L2", story_text=story
    )
    assert res.status == "pass"

No other changes. Run test_peer_social_validator.py to verify.

ChatGPT said:
This is good work. The implementation matches the spec, the scope is correct, and nothing unsafe crept in. 👍 Here’s a clean review + a couple of small, high-value refinements before we lock it.

✅ What’s correct (no changes needed)
1. Architectural placement
✅ Validator runs offline / prebuild, not at runtime.
✅ No changes to NarrativeFlow, StoryLoader, or assembly.
✅ Pure function + CLI hook = perfect.
2. Behavioral correctness
✅ Persona-gated (Gen Z / Gen Alpha only)
✅ Topic-gated (socially relevant topics only)
✅ Tier-aware:
L1 → WARN
L2/L3 → FAIL
✅ Deterministic, no inference, no mutation
3. Tests
✅ Minimal but sufficient
✅ Covers:
pass
fail
warn
non-applicable persona
non-applicable topic
This is exactly the level of enforcement we wanted.

🔧 Two small refinements I strongly recommend
These are not redesigns — they just make the rule safer and more future-proof.

🔧 1. Make peer_social_context_required explicit (schema-driven override)
Right now the rule is implicitly derived from persona + topic. That’s fine as a default, but we already said:
“If we want exceptions, they must be explicit in the plan/spec.”
Add one optional flag to the validator input:


def validate_story_social_context(
    *,
    persona: str,
    topic: str,
    tier: Tier,
    story_text: str,
    peer_social_context_required: Optional[bool] = None,
) -> ValidationResult:

Then adjust logic:


if peer_social_context_required is False:
    return ValidationResult(
        status="pass",
        message="Social-context rule explicitly disabled by spec.",
    )

needs_social = (
    peer_social_context_required is True
    or (_is_social_persona(persona) and _is_social_topic(topic))
)

Why this matters:
Lets the plan or story spec override defaults cleanly
Prevents future “why did this fail?” confusion
Aligns with your explicit planning > inference principle
This does not change current behavior unless someone opts out.

🔧 2. Slightly tighten the regex without NLP
Right now group alone may false-positive in some contexts.
I suggest splitting patterns into:
actors (friend, coworker, boss…)
reactions (encouraged, noticed, reacted…)
And require at least one of each OR one strong phrase.
Minimal tweak:


SOCIAL_ACTORS = [
    r"friend",
    r"peer",
    r"classmate",
    r"coworker",
    r"teammate",
    r"boss",
    r"team",
]

SOCIAL_REACTIONS = [
    r"encouraged",
    r"reacted",
    r"laughed",
    r"noticed",
    r"said",
    r"told me",
]

ACTOR_REGEX = re.compile("|".join(SOCIAL_ACTORS), re.I)
REACTION_REGEX = re.compile("|".join(SOCIAL_REACTIONS), re.I)

def _has_social_indicator(text: str) -> bool:
    return bool(ACTOR_REGEX.search(text) and REACTION_REGEX.search(text))

This:
Still keeps it cheap + deterministic
Reduces accidental passes
Avoids NLP complexity
Optional, but recommended.

🚦 Final verdict
Approved with minor refinements recommended.
You can reply to dev with this:

Reply you can send to dev (copy/paste):
This is correct and approved. The behavior, scope, and tests match the spec exactly.
Before we lock it, please do two small refinements:
Add an optional peer_social_context_required boolean override to the validator input so plans/specs can explicitly enable/disable the rule.
Slightly tighten the regex by requiring an actor + reaction match (still heuristic, no NLP).
No other changes needed. After that, wire it into the story prebuild pipeline and we’re done.

Once that’s merged, this rule is done and we move on.

                                           topic="confidence",
                                           tier="L2",
                                           story_text="...")

"""

    "identity",
    "behavior",
    "behavior_change",
    "habits",
    "confidence",
    "motivation",
    "belonging",
    "social_pressure",
    "self_improvement",
    "stress",
}

    r"friend",
    r"peer",
    r"classmate",
    r"coworker",
    r"teammate",
    r"boss",
    r"group",

    r"they encouraged",
    r"they laughed",
    r"they noticed",
    r"my team",
    r"at work",
    r"at school",

SOCIAL_REGEX = re.compile("|".join(SOCIAL_PATTERNS), flags=re.IGNORECASE)


@dataclass
class ValidationResult:
    status: Literal["pass", "warn", "fail"]
    message: str


def _is_social_persona(persona: str) -> bool:

    *,
    persona: str,

    tier: Tier,
    story_text: str,

    if has_social:

        status="fail",
        message="L2/L3 story missing required peer/social reaction.",
    )


# Convenience CLI hook (optional)

    import argparse
    parser = argparse.ArgumentParser(description="Validate peer/social context in a story.")
    parser.add_argument("--persona", required=True, help="Persona name (e.g., 'Gen Z').")
    parser.add_argument("--topic", required=True, help="Topic name (e.g., 'confidence').")
    parser.add_argument("--tier", required=True, choices=["L1", "L2", "L3"], help="Story tier.")

        persona=args.persona, topic=args.topic, tier=args.tier, story_text=text
    )

    raise SystemExit(main())

from zencoder.rules.peer_social_validator import validate_story_social_context


def test_pass_when_social_present():
    story = "Her friend encouraged her after the presentation."
    res = validate_story_social_context(
        persona="Gen Z", topic="confidence", tier="L2", story_text=story
    )
    assert res.status == "pass"


def test_fail_when_missing_social_in_l2():
    story = "She worked alone and eventually felt better."
    res = validate_story_social_context(
        persona="Gen Z", topic="confidence", tier="L2", story_text=story
    )
    assert res.status == "fail"


def test_warn_when_missing_social_in_l1():
    story = "He took a breath and started again."
    res = validate_story_social_context(
        persona="Gen Z", topic="confidence", tier="L1", story_text=story
    )
    assert res.status == "warn"


def test_not_applicable_other_persona():
    story = "He practiced alone."
    res = validate_story_social_context(
        persona="Baby Boomer", topic="confidence", tier="L2", story_text=story
    )
    assert res.status == "pass"


def test_not_applicable_other_topic():
    story = "He practiced alone."
    res = validate_story_social_context(
        persona="Gen Z", topic="finance", tier="L2", story_text=story
    )
    assert res.status == "pass"

This is **good work**. The implementation matches the spec, the scope is correct, and nothing unsafe crept in. 👍
Here’s a **clean review + a couple of small, high-value refinements** before we lock it.

---

## ✅ What’s correct (no changes needed)

### 1. Architectural placement

* ✅ Validator runs **offline / prebuild**, not at runtime.
* ✅ No changes to NarrativeFlow, StoryLoader, or assembly.
* ✅ Pure function + CLI hook = perfect.

### 2. Behavioral correctness

* ✅ Persona-gated (Gen Z / Gen Alpha only)
* ✅ Topic-gated (socially relevant topics only)
* ✅ Tier-aware:

  * L1 → WARN
  * L2/L3 → FAIL
* ✅ Deterministic, no inference, no mutation

### 3. Tests

* ✅ Minimal but sufficient
* ✅ Covers:

  * pass
  * fail
  * warn
  * non-applicable persona
  * non-applicable topic

This is exactly the level of enforcement we wanted.

---

## 🔧 Two small refinements I strongly recommend

These are **not redesigns** — they just make the rule safer and more future-proof.

---

### 🔧 1. Make peer_social_context_required explicit (schema-driven override)

Right now the rule is **implicitly derived** from persona + topic.
That’s fine as a default, but we already said:

> “If we want exceptions, they must be explicit in the plan/spec.”

**Add one optional flag** to the validator input:
python
def validate_story_social_context(
    *,
    persona: str,
    topic: str,
    tier: Tier,
    story_text: str,
    peer_social_context_required: Optional[bool] = None,
) -> ValidationResult:

Then adjust logic:
python
if peer_social_context_required is False:
    return ValidationResult(
        status="pass",
        message="Social-context rule explicitly disabled by spec.",
    )

needs_social = (
    peer_social_context_required is True
    or (_is_social_persona(persona) and _is_social_topic(topic))
)

Why this matters:

* Lets the **plan or story spec override** defaults cleanly
* Prevents future “why did this fail?” confusion
* Aligns with your *explicit planning > inference* principle

This does **not** change current behavior unless someone opts out.

---

### 🔧 2. Slightly tighten the regex without NLP

Right now group alone may false-positive in some contexts.

I suggest splitting patterns into:

* **actors** (friend, coworker, boss…)
* **reactions** (encouraged, noticed, reacted…)

And require **at least one of each OR one strong phrase**.

Minimal tweak:
python
SOCIAL_ACTORS = [
    r"friend",
    r"peer",
    r"classmate",
    r"coworker",
    r"teammate",
    r"boss",
    r"team",
]

SOCIAL_REACTIONS = [
    r"encouraged",
    r"reacted",
    r"laughed",
    r"noticed",
    r"said",
    r"told me",
]

ACTOR_REGEX = re.compile("|".join(SOCIAL_ACTORS), re.I)
REACTION_REGEX = re.compile("|".join(SOCIAL_REACTIONS), re.I)

def _has_social_indicator(text: str) -> bool:
    return bool(ACTOR_REGEX.search(text) and REACTION_REGEX.search(text))

This:

* Still keeps it **cheap + deterministic**
* Reduces accidental passes
* Avoids NLP complexity

Optional, but recommended.

---

## 🚦 Final verdict

**Approved with minor refinements recommended.**

# Regex-friendly indicators of social reactions
SOCIAL_PATTERNS: List[str] = [
SOCIAL_ACTORS: List[str] = [
    r"friend",
    r"boss",
    r"team",
    r"group",
    r"they reacted",
    r"they encouraged",
    r"they laughed",
    r"they noticed",
    r"my team",
    r"at work",
    r"at school",
]

SOCIAL_REGEX = re.compile("|".join(SOCIAL_PATTERNS), flags=re.IGNORECASE)
SOCIAL_REACTIONS: List[str] = [
    r"encouraged",
    r"reacted",
    r"laughed",
    r"noticed",
    r"said",
    r"told me",
    r"pushed back",
    r"cheered",
]

ACTOR_REGEX = re.compile("|".join(SOCIAL_ACTORS), flags=re.IGNORECASE)
REACTION_REGEX = re.compile("|".join(SOCIAL_REACTIONS), flags=re.IGNORECASE)

def _has_social_indicator(text: str) -> bool:
    return bool(SOCIAL_REGEX.search(text or ""))
    if not text:
        return False
    return bool(ACTOR_REGEX.search(text) and REACTION_REGEX.search(text))

Based on the context from our previous work on Phoenix Omega, I can describe the teacher story/exercise components:

## Teacher Story & Exercise Components

### 1. **Teacher Persona Framework**
- **Expert Teacher**: A knowledgeable guide who provides context, wisdom, and structured learning
- **Teaching Modes**: 
  - off - No explicit teacher voice
  - prefer - Teacher voice preferred when available
  - strict - Strict adherence to teacher content

### 2. **Exercise Family Structure**
Exercises are organized by type and intensity:

#### **Exercise Types**
- **Cognitive Exercises** (Chapter 7): Mental reframing and perspective shifts
- **Pranayama Exercises** (Chapters 4, 7, 8): Breath-based practices
- **Grounding Exercises** (Chapter 7): Physical anchoring techniques

#### **Intensity Levels**
- **L1 (Level 1)**: Foundational practices (Chapters 4, 7)
- **L2 (Level 2)**: Advanced practices (Chapter 8)

### 3. **Exercise Components per Chapter**

#### **Chapter 4** - Introduction to Breath
- **Exercise 1**: Basic pranayama (L1, 3 cycles)
- **Purpose Statement**: Why this practice matters
- **Integration Bridge**: Connecting practice to daily life
- **Coexistence Test**: Checking if emotion disappears vs. breathing with it

#### **Chapter 7** - Deepening Practice
- **Exercise 1 (Cognitive)**: 3-step reframing process
- **Exercise 2 (Pranayama)**: Breath practice (L1, 3 cycles)
- **Exercise 3 (Grounding)**: Physical anchoring (L1)

#### **Chapter 8** - Advanced Techniques
- **Exercise 1 (Extended Exhale)**: 4-6 count breathing (L2)
- **Exercise 2 (Feet Grounding)**: Physical anchoring (L2)
- **Exercise 3 (4-7-8 Breath)**: Advanced pattern (L2)

### 4. **Story Integration**
The teacher story weaves through:
- **Context Setting**: Why each practice matters
- **Progression**: Building from simple to complex
- **Application**: How to use practices in real situations
- **Wisdom**: Deeper insights about emotional coexistence

### 5. **Variable Structure**
Each exercise has standardized variables:
- purpose_statement: Why we're doing this
- framing: How to approach the practice
- steps[]: Sequential instructions
- integration_bridge: Connecting practice to life
- name: Display name for the exercise

This structure ensures consistent, progressive learning while maintaining the Phoenix Omega principle of deterministic template-based generation. ## Key Files & Functions for Teacher Story/Exercise System

## How the System Gets Teacher Stories & Exercises

### **1. Writer Input Process**

#### **Writer Assignment**
yaml
# /SOURCE_OF_TRUTH/data/writer_assignment.yaml
persona_id: "gen_z_professionals"

chapters:

#### **Writer Creates Content**
Writers fill in atom files with specific exercise and story variables:

yaml
# /writer_submissions/chapter_04_atoms.yaml
exercise_1:
  purpose_statement: "This practice teaches you that emotions and breath can coexist"
  framing: "You don't need to wait for anxiety to disappear before breathing"
  name: "Basic Coexistence Breath"
  integration_bridge: "Notice if the anxiety vanished or if you breathed with it present"

teacher_wisdom:
  breath_foundation: "Breath is your anchor in any emotional storm"
  coexistence_principle: "You don't need to fix feelings to live with them"

### **2. Compilation Process**

#### **Scoped Compilation**
python
# /SOURCE_OF_TRUTH/tools/compile_writer_atoms_scoped.py
def merge_values_scoped(base, new, chapter_num, source):
    for key, value in new.items():
        # Prefix exercise variables with chapter number
        scoped_key = f"ch{chapter_num}_{key}" if key.startswith('exercise_') else key
        base[scoped_key] = value

**Result**: exercise_1 becomes ch4_exercise_1 to avoid conflicts with other chapters

### **3. Template Resolution**

#### **CSV Template Contains Placeholders**
csv
chapter,order,section_key,template_text
4,8,exercise_1,"{exercise_1.purpose_statement}
[GUIDED PRACTICE: {exercise_1.name} - L1 intensity - 3 cycles]
{teacher_wisdom.breath_foundation}"

#### **Generation Resolves Tokens**
python
# /SOURCE_OF_TRUTH/tools/generate_phoenix_omega_book_scoped.py
def resolve_token_value(token, values, chapter):
    # For chapter 4, exercise_1 becomes ch4_exercise_1
    if parts[0].startswith('exercise_') and chapter is not None:
        scoped_base = f"ch{chapter}_{parts[0]}"
        current = values[scoped_base]
    return current

### **4. Validation Rules**

#### **Exercise Placement Validation**
python
# /SOURCE_OF_TRUTH/tools/validate_phoenix_omega_template.py
def validate_chapter_permissions(csv_data):
    # Chapter 4: exactly 1 exercise at order 8
    # Chapter 7: exactly 3 exercises at orders 5,6,7
    # Chapter 8: exactly 3 exercises at orders 4,7,9

#### **Required Markers**
python
def validate_exercise_beats(csv_data):
    # Chapter 4, order 8 must contain:
    # - {exercise_1.purpose_statement}
    # - [GUIDED PRACTICE: {exercise_1.name} - L1 intensity - 3 cycles]

### **5. Final Output Generation**

#### **Hydrated Content**
markdown
## Chapter 4: Learning to Breathe With Anxiety

### Exercise 1: Basic Coexistence Breath

This practice teaches you that emotions and breath can coexist.

[GUIDED PRACTICE: Basic Coexistence Breath - L1 intensity - 3 cycles]

Breath is your anchor in any emotional storm.

### Integration Bridge
Notice if the anxiety vanished or if you breathed with it present.

### **6. Data Flow Summary**

Writer Assignment
       ↓
Writer fills chapter_XX_atoms.yaml
       ↓
compile_writer_atoms_scoped.py
       ↓
omega_values_scoped.yaml (with ch4_exercise_1, ch7_exercise_1, etc.)
       ↓
generate_phoenix_omega_book_scoped.py
       ↓
Resolves tokens from phoenix_omega_book_template.csv
       ↓
Final book with teacher stories and exercises

### **Key Points**

1. **Writers provide content** - They write the actual exercise instructions and teacher wisdom
2. **Templates provide structure** - CSV defines where exercises appear and their format
3. **Compilation prevents conflicts** - Variables are scoped by chapter number
4. **Validation ensures quality** - Rules enforce correct exercise placement and content
5. **Generation is deterministic** - Same inputs always produce same output

This system ensures teacher stories and exercises are consistently placed, properly formatted, and free of variable conflicts while maintaining writer creativity within the structured framework.

You said:
Here’s a clean way to adapt “teacher mode” to Phoenix Omega V4 while keeping V4’s frozen‑plan, lookup‑only contract.

Goal
Every story/section is framed as: “Author learned this from Teacher and is now sharing it with you.”

What to add (plan/schema level)

In each V4 plan (per chapter/section), add metadata flags:
teacher_mode: true
teacher_id: <canon teacher> (optional but recommended for consistency)
teacher_provenance_required: true
In story_requirements (the extractor you’re adding), carry through:
teacher_mode (bool)
teacher_id (string, optional)
teacher_provenance_required (bool)
Story bank expectations (prewrite stage)

When generating stories for slots that have teacher_provenance_required:
Require a teacher provenance beat in each tier (L1/L2/L3) of the same story family.
Minimal template for the beat:
L1: 1–2 sentences: “My teacher showed me… I’m passing it to you.”
L2/L3: Short setup + transmission + how it was applied.
Add metadata to each story JSON:
teacher_mode: true
teacher_id: <id> (if specified)
provenance_line: short string used for validation/logging.
Section/variant side (V4 text assets)

In section variants where teacher voice should surface (HOOK/TEACHING/SCENE/REFLECTION), ensure the variants already have a {teacher_provenance} placeholder or a neutral slot where the injected story text can carry it.
If no placeholder exists, rely on the story text itself to include the provenance beat—keep section variants untouched to honor frozen assets.
Assembler / injector behavior

Keep assembler lookup-only. No runtime authoring.
At story selection time:
Filter candidate stories to those with teacher_mode == true when the plan/section requires it.
If teacher_id is present in plan, prefer exact teacher_id; otherwise fall back to any teacher_mode story.
If no eligible story → hard fail (consistent with V4’s “cannot be wrong by construction” principle).
Validators (build-time, not runtime)

Extend your story validator (or add a small check alongside the peer validator) to assert:
If teacher_provenance_required is true, story text contains a provenance beat (regex like (my teacher|our teacher|learned from)), and metadata teacher_mode is true.
If teacher_id is specified in plan, the selected story’s teacher_id matches.
Fail on L2/L3; warn on L1 (mirrors the peer-rule severity pattern).
Minimal touch points

Plan schema: add flags (teacher_mode, teacher_id, teacher_provenance_required).
Story requirements extractor: pass those flags through.
Prewrite generator: produce teacher-mode stories with provenance beat + metadata.
StoryLoader filter (in story_injector_v4): prefer/require teacher_mode + teacher_id when requested.
Validator: provenance present + teacher_id match.
What not to change

Don’t alter V4 section text dynamically at assemble time.
Don’t add runtime generation.
Keep deterministic selection; no heuristic rewriting.
If you want, I can draft the small filter patch for story_injector_v4.py and a validator snippet parallel to the peer validator.

good. desc to me how this becomes a book about a teacher's stuff where author is sharing? desc the teacher inputs that make this intent happen

Here’s how the V4 book becomes “I learned this from my teacher and I’m sharing it with you,” and what inputs make it happen:

Narrative spine (how it shows up in the book)

Each story that gets injected carries a short provenance beat: “My teacher showed me…” / “I learned this from… and here’s how you can use it.” That beat travels with the story text itself, so the section prose stays frozen.
When the assembler picks a story for a section that is marked teacher-mode, it only selects stories tagged teacher_mode=true (and, if set, the specific teacher_id). So every inserted story already encodes the “teacher → author → you” framing.
The section variants remain unchanged; the teacher framing comes from the story text + metadata, not runtime rewriting.
Inputs you provide to make this intent happen

Plan-level flags (per section or per book)
teacher_mode: true
teacher_id: <canonical teacher> (optional but recommended for consistency)
teacher_provenance_required: true
Story requirements
Carry the above flags into story_requirements.json so prewriting knows these slots demand teacher-mode stories.
Story bank entries (prewritten stories)
Story JSON includes:
teacher_mode: true
teacher_id: <id> (if specified)
Provenance line embedded in each tier (L1/L2/L3) of the story family (e.g., 1–2 sentences in L1, richer handoff in L2/L3).
Selector filter
Story injector filters candidates to teacher_mode=true, and matches teacher_id when provided; if none found, build fails (no runtime fallback).
Validator check (prebuild)
If teacher_provenance_required is true: story text must contain a teacher provenance phrase and metadata must mark teacher_mode=true (and teacher_id matches if given). Fail for L2/L3, warn for L1.
Result in the book

Every story slot marked for teacher mode will read as the author passing along what their teacher taught, because the provenance text is inside the story itself and only teacher-tagged stories are eligible for those slots. No extra runtime narration is needed—determinism and frozen assets remain intact.

Here’s the simple, plain-English version, stripped to the core.

What “teacher mode” means in the book
In teacher mode, the book reads as:
“I learned this from my teacher, and I’m now sharing it with you.”
The author is not claiming original authority. They are explicitly acting as a transmitter of a teacher’s wisdom.
That framing shows up inside the stories themselves, not by rewriting the book at runtime.

How this works in Phoenix Omega V4 (simply)
1. You tell the plan: “this section is teacher mode”
In the V4 plan, you mark sections (or the whole book) with:
teacher_mode: true
optionally: teacher_id: <name>
teacher_provenance_required: true
This is the intent signal.

2. Stories are prewritten with teacher provenance
Before books are assembled:
The story engine prewrites stories that include a short line like:
“My teacher showed me this…”
“I learned this from my teacher, and here’s how I use it…”
Each story is tagged with metadata:
teacher_mode: true
teacher_id (if specified)
This happens offline, once.

3. Assembly only selects teacher stories
When V4 builds the book:
If a section is marked teacher mode:
the assembler only selects stories tagged teacher_mode=true
if a teacher_id is set, it must match
If no matching story exists → hard fail
No writing. No editing. Just lookup.

4. The book naturally reads as “teacher → author → reader”
Because:
every injected story already contains the provenance line
every story was filtered to teacher mode
section text stays frozen
The result feels consistent across the book:
the teacher is the source
the author is the messenger
the reader is the recipient

What inputs make this happen (summary)
You provide:
Plan flags (teacher_mode, teacher_id, teacher_provenance_required)
Prewritten teacher-mode stories (with provenance text + metadata)

What does not happen (by design)
❌ No runtime rewriting
❌ No dynamic narration
❌ No changing section text
❌ No guessing or heuristics
Everything is planned, prewritten, validated, and deterministic.

In Phoenix Omega V4, a “teacher book” works by only allowing prewritten stories that explicitly say “I learned this from my teacher” to appear in teacher-marked sections—so the author naturally reads as passing the teacher’s wisdom forward.

The story injector filters to teacher_mode=true (and matching teacher_id if set).
If no eligible story exists → hard fail.
Validator enforces:
provenance present when required
metadata matches
L2/L3 = fail, L1 = warn if missing

Result
Every teacher-marked story reads as the author passing along what their teacher taught, consistently across the book. No runtime generation, no text mutation, no heuristics — fully deterministic and frozen.

One-line takeaway:
In Omega V4, a “teacher book” works by only allowing prewritten stories that explicitly say “I learned this from my teacher” to appear in teacher-marked sections, so the author is clearly sharing—not claiming—authority.

How Phoenix Raising handled different book durations
Core idea
Phoenix Raising does NOT plan different books structurally for different lengths. It uses the same core content, then scales duration by pacing and repetition, not by adding/removing logic.

The three duration modes (conceptual)
Phoenix Raising supports different “durations” (short / standard / long), but they are implemented as render-time scaling, not planning-time changes.
1. Short duration
Fewer repetitions
Shorter pauses
Minimal reflections
Exercises often appear once
Stories are brief (L1-style usage)
2. Standard duration
Default pacing
Normal reflection blocks
Exercises appear as designed
Balanced story + instruction
3. Long duration
More repetition
Slower pacing
Extended reflections
Exercises may be revisited
Stories may be followed by additional commentary

NarrativeFlow pacing rules
Loop counts
Reflection density

What does NOT change
❌ Story selection logic
❌ Story content
❌ Exercise content
❌ Planning
❌ Validation rules

Content planned per section

Plan YAML decides

How to approximate Phoenix Raising–style duration in Omega V4
(without breaking determinism or the frozen-plan rule)
Core principle
In V4, duration is not pacing at runtime. It’s achieved by choosing different prewritten section variants at plan time.
So instead of:
“Stay longer with the same content”
V4 does:
“Include more (or fewer) content blocks that already exist.”

The safe V4 mapping (Raising → V4)
What Raising did
Same story/exercise
More repetition, reflection, commentary
Slower emotional arc
All decided at render time
What V4 must do instead
Never repeat content
Never add runtime logic
Never scale loops
So V4 approximates duration by structural density, not pacing.

The V4 mechanism: Duration via section variants
1. Duration is a plan attribute
In the V4 plan:


duration: short | standard | long

This value is read-only during assembly.

2. Each section has duration-aware variants
Example: REFLECTION section variants


variants:
  short:
    - reflection_compact_v1
  standard:
    - reflection_standard_v1
    - reflection_standard_v2
  long:
    - reflection_extended_v1
    - reflection_extended_v2

All variants are:
Prewritten
Validated
Deterministic

3. What actually changes by duration
Element
Short
Standard
Long
Reflection length
1–2 paragraphs
2–3
4–5
Teacher commentary
Minimal
Present
Rich
Story tier used
L1 only
L1/L2
L1/L2/L3
Integration language
Light
Normal
Deep
No repeats. No loops. Just different assets.

Stories are not duplicated.
Instead:

Standard → inject L1 or L2
Long → inject L2 or L3
Still:
Same story family

Deterministic selection
This mirrors Raising’s “longer time with material” feeling.

Explanation length
Setup framing
Integration text

exercise_variant:

  long: exercise_setup_extended

The exercise itself is identical.

All of that violates V4’s guarantees.

Mental model (important)
Phoenix Raising
“Stay longer with the same moment.”
Phoenix Omega V4
“Include more moments that explore the same truth.”
That’s why V4 is safer at scale.

Here’s a clear, accurate description of the different ways Phoenix Raising achieved platform uniqueness and spam avoidance, especially when using fewer chapters or fewer sections.

How Phoenix Raising handled uniqueness with fewer chapters/sections
Core constraint Phoenix Raising worked under
Phoenix Raising often produced:
Short books
Audio-first content
Few chapters (sometimes 3–6)
Reused the same underlying CSV content
So uniqueness could not come from structure alone.

1. Chapter order shuffling (soft re-sequencing)
Phoenix Raising did not lock chapter order.
Chapters were treated as modules
NarrativeFlow could:
Move grounding earlier
Delay exercises
Front-load recognition
The arc stayed intact, but the order shifted
Example:
Book A: Recognition → Exercise → Story → Reflection
Book B: Story → Recognition → Exercise → Reflection
This alone dramatically reduced platform fingerprinting.
➡️ Omega V4 cannot do this (order is frozen).

2. Beat-level rearrangement inside chapters
Even within a single chapter:
Beats (sentences/paragraphs) were not fixed
NarrativeFlow assembled:
Recognition beats
Shift beats
Anchor beats in different internal orders
Same content, different flow.
This means:
Two books could share 90% of atoms
But never share the same paragraph sequence
➡️ V4 replaces this with section variants, not reordering.

3. Variable chapter counts per book
Phoenix Raising allowed:
3-chapter books
5-chapter books
7-chapter books
Chapters were optional, not mandatory.
Example:
Some books skipped grounding
Others skipped deep reflection
Some had no exercises at all
This made platform similarity detection much harder.
➡️ V4 uses different plans, not optional chapters.

4. Style pack rotation (big one)
Phoenix Raising relied heavily on style variance:
Teacher
Peer
Poetic
Direct
Clinical
Encouraging
Reflective
The same sentence could be rendered in 10+ styles via:


somatic_generic_with_10_styles.csv

This was a huge uniqueness multiplier even with short content.
➡️ In V4, this becomes section-level voice variants.

5. Story density variation
Phoenix Raising varied:
Whether stories appeared at all
How many stories per chapter
Whether stories were short (L1-like) or extended
Some books:
Had zero stories Others:
Had multiple short anecdotes
This changed the “shape” of the text.
➡️ V4 encodes this as story_eligible flags + tiers.

6. Exercise presence as a uniqueness lever
Exercises were optional and variable:
Some books: no exercises
Some: one breath practice
Some: several
And exercises were:
Framed differently
Introduced differently
Integrated differently
Even identical exercises looked different.
➡️ V4 fixes placement but varies exercise framing variants.

7. Duration as repetition, not content
Longer Raising books were not longer structurally. They:
Revisited ideas
Rephrased insights
Re-guided the same practice
This created uniqueness without adding content.
➡️ V4 must add new sections, not repetition.

8. NarrativeFlow anti-periodicity rules
Phoenix Raising explicitly tracked:
Recently used atoms
Recent phrase patterns
Recent story usage
And avoided:
Repeating the same opening
Repeating the same beat pattern
Reusing the same story too often
This was runtime intelligence.
➡️ V4 replaces this with simulation + coverage gates.

Why this matters for Omega V4
Phoenix Raising achieved uniqueness by:
Runtime rearrangement
Repetition control
Style rotation
Optional structure
Omega V4 must achieve the same effect using:
Multiple plans
Section variants
Story tiers
Coverage rules
Simulation-driven expansion

One-sentence takeaway
Phoenix Raising avoided platform spam with runtime reordering, style rotation, optional chapters, and beat-level variation—Omega V4 must replace all of that with explicit plans, variants, and coverage gates.

Selection remains deterministic (seed + section) over the filtered teacher-story pool.

Created
teacher_bank_loader.py
#!/usr/bin/env python3
"""
Teacher Bank Loader

Read-only loader for teacher-specific reusable stories (and future exercises).
Path layout:
  SOURCE_OF_TRUTH/data/teacher_banks/<teacher_id>/stories/*.json

Only supports stories for now, indexed by (teacher_id, persona, topic, layer).
"""

import json
from pathlib import Path
from typing import Dict, List, Optional, Any


class TeacherBankLoaderError(Exception):
    """Raised when teacher bank loading fails."""
    pass


class TeacherBankLoader:
    """Read-only loader for teacher-mode stories."""

    def __init__(self, bank_root: Path):
        self.bank_root = Path(bank_root)
        if not self.bank_root.exists():
            raise TeacherBankLoaderError(f"Teacher bank root not found: {bank_root}")
        self._stories: Dict[str, Dict[str, Any]] = {}
        self._index: Dict[str, List[str]] = {}
        self._load_all_stories()

    def _load_all_stories(self) -> None:
        for teacher_dir in self.bank_root.glob("*"):
            if not teacher_dir.is_dir():
                continue
            teacher_id = teacher_dir.name
            stories_dir = teacher_dir / "stories"
            if not stories_dir.exists():
                continue
            for json_file in stories_dir.glob("*.json"):
                try:
                    data = json.load(json_file.open())
                    story_id = data.get("story_id") or json_file.stem
                    data["teacher_id"] = data.get("teacher_id", teacher_id)
                    self._stories[story_id] = data
                    persona = data.get("persona", "universal")
                    topic = data.get("topic", "")
                    layers = data.get("layers", data.get("tiers", {}))
                    for layer in ["L1", "L2", "L3"]:
                        if layer in layers:
                            key = self._make_key(persona, topic, layer, data["teacher_id"])
                            self._index.setdefault(key, []).append(story_id)
                except Exception:
                    # Skip bad files silently to avoid failing whole load
                    continue

    @staticmethod
    def _make_key(persona: str, topic: str, layer: str, teacher_id: str) -> str:
        return f"{teacher_id}::{persona}::{topic}::{layer}"

    def list_stories(
        self,
        persona: str,
        topic: str,
        layer: str,
        teacher_id: Optional[str] = None
    ) -> List[str]:
        """List story_ids matching filters."""
        keys = []
        tid = teacher_id or "*"
        # Exact teacher if provided
        if teacher_id:
            keys.append(self._make_key(persona, topic, layer, teacher_id))
        else:
            # Aggregate all teachers for this persona/topic/layer
            for tid_candidate in {data.get("teacher_id", "") for data in self._stories.values()}:
                if tid_candidate:
                    keys.append(self._make_key(persona, topic, layer, tid_candidate))
        out: List[str] = []
        for k in keys:
            out.extend(self._index.get(k, []))
        return list(set(out))

    def get_story_by_id(self, story_id: str) -> Dict[str, Any]:
        if story_id not in self._stories:
            raise TeacherBankLoaderError(f"Story not found: {story_id}")
        return self._stories[story_id]

    def load_story(self, story_id: str, layer: str) -> str:
        story = self.get_story_by_id(story_id)
        layers = story.get("layers", story.get("tiers", {}))
        if layer not in layers:
            raise TeacherBankLoaderError(f"Layer {layer} not found in story {story_id}")
        layer_data = layers[layer]
        if isinstance(layer_data, dict):
            text = layer_data.get("text", "")
        else:
            text = str(layer_data)
        if not text:
            raise TeacherBankLoaderError(f"Empty text in story {story_id} layer {layer}")
        return text

ChatGPT said:
Here’s a simple, accurate description of how Phoenix Raising (pre-Omega) handled spam avoidance and uniqueness by varying chapter sequence, chapter count, and section mix, without breaking meaning.

Core idea (Phoenix Raising)
Phoenix Raising treated a book as a modular composition, not a fixed outline.
The goal was:
Many structurally different books that teach the same thing, so platforms don’t see them as duplicates.
This was done before Phoenix Omega froze plans.

1. Variable chapter count (short / medium / long books)
Phoenix Raising supported multiple “durations” of the same book idea:
Short (e.g. 5–6 chapters)
Standard (8–10 chapters)
Extended (12–15 chapters)
How it worked:
Each topic had a chapter pool, not a single outline.
A duration preset selected:
Which chapters were included
Which were omitted
Which were merged
Example:
Short book: skip deep theory chapters
Long book: include reflection + integration chapters
This meant:
Same topic ≠ same chapter list
Platform sees different TOCs and lengths

2. Chapter reordering (sequence variation)
Phoenix Raising allowed multiple valid chapter orders.
Rules:
Some chapters had ordering constraints (e.g. intro must be first)
Others were order-flexible
Example:
Emotional awareness could come before or after breathwork
Reflection chapters could appear mid-book or near the end
Mechanism:
Chapter graph, not a fixed list
Generator picked a valid path through the graph
Result:
Books taught the same ideas
But the learning journey felt different
TOC fingerprints differed strongly

3. Section mix inside chapters (internal uniqueness)
Even within the same chapter theme, Phoenix Raising varied section composition.
A chapter was assembled from a section menu, such as:
Story
Teaching explanation
Exercise
Reflection
Prompt
Case example
Rules:
Minimum / maximum sections enforced
Certain combinations disallowed
Order could vary
Example:
Chapter A: Story → Teaching → Exercise
Chapter B: Teaching → Story → Reflection
Chapter C: Story → Exercise → Teaching
So even if two books had a “Breathing with Anxiety” chapter:
The internal structure differed
Paragraph-level similarity dropped sharply

4. Content substitution (not rewriting)
Phoenix Raising did substitution, not generation tricks:
Multiple stories for the same slot
Multiple exercises with same goal
Multiple explanations of same concept
The system:
Picked one per slot
Never blended or rewrote at runtime
This preserved:
Human-written quality
Determinism (seed-based)
Clean validation

5. Why this reduced platform spam risk
Platforms fingerprint:
Chapter count
Chapter titles
Order
Section density
Structural rhythm
Phoenix Raising changed all of those, even when:
Topic was the same
Audience was similar
Lessons were identical
So two books:
Were pedagogically equivalent
But structurally distinct enough to avoid clustering

How this differs from Phoenix Omega V4
Phoenix Raising
Flexible structure
Variable chapter count
Variable order
Section-level assembly
Optimized for scale & spam resistance
Phoenix Omega V4
Frozen plans
Fixed chapter count
Fixed section order
Optimized for correctness, determinism, and compliance
This is why:
Phoenix Raising handled platform spam avoidance
Phoenix Omega handles “cannot be wrong by construction”

Key takeaway
Phoenix Raising avoided spam by changing structure, not words:
Different chapter counts
Different chapter sequences
Different section mixes
Same underlying wisdom
That architectural idea is still valid — Omega just freezes it earlier in the pipeline.
If you want, next we can:
Map which of these ideas can safely return in V4 planning, or
Design a V4-safe “multi-plan” system that keeps Omega guarantees while regaining Phoenix Raising diversity.

You said:
desc how omega did it, if different

ChatGPT said:
Here’s the clean, simple explanation of how Phoenix Omega did it, and how it differs from Phoenix Raising.

Core idea (Phoenix Omega)
Phoenix Omega does NOT vary structure at generation time.
Instead, it achieves uniqueness by:
Freezing many different valid plans up front, then generating deterministically from each one.
So diversity exists — but only before generation starts.

1. Fixed chapter count per plan (no runtime variation)
Unlike Phoenix Raising, Omega does not change chapter count on the fly.
Instead:
Each plan has a fixed chapter count (e.g. always 12)
Different plans may exist for the same topic:
Plan A: 12 chapters
Plan B: 9 chapters
Plan C: 6 chapters
Key rule:
Once a plan is selected, nothing about chapter count can change.
So:
No merging
No skipping
No conditional chapters

2. Fixed chapter order (no graph traversal)
Phoenix Omega removes reordering entirely after planning.
In Omega:
Chapter order is locked in the plan
Section order inside chapters is locked
There is no runtime “valid path” logic
Uniqueness comes from:
Having multiple pre-authored plans
Not from reordering within a plan
This satisfies:
Determinism
Validator guarantees
“Cannot be wrong by construction”

3. Section diversity comes from slot content, not structure
Omega allows variation inside slots, not in layout.
What varies:
Which story family is selected
Which tier (L1/L2/L3) is used
Which exercise variant is selected
Which wording variant of a section is chosen
What does not vary:
Number of sections
Section sequence
Section types per chapter
So two Omega books:
Have identical skeletons
But different flesh

4. Uniqueness via preplanned multiplicity (not assembly tricks)
Omega shifts all variability earlier:
Phoenix Raising
1 plan
Many runtime assembly paths
Phoenix Omega
Many plans
1 runtime path per plan
Each plan is:
Fully validated
Fully simulated
Known-good before use
This is why Omega can run:
1k / 10k simulations
Collision analysis
Hard gates

5. Spam resistance strategy in Omega
Omega relies on plan diversity + content diversity, not structural mutation.
It avoids platform clustering by:
Multiple canonical plans per topic/persona
Tier variation (L1 vs L3 stories)
Different story families
Different exercises
Different phrasing variants
But never by:
Changing chapter count mid-run
Reordering sections
Dropping content conditionally

6. Why Omega is stricter than Phoenix Raising
Omega trades flexibility for safety.
Benefits:
Deterministic output
Strong validation
Reproducibility
Easier compliance review
Cost:
Less structural variety per plan
Requires more upfront planning work
This is intentional.

One-line summary
Phoenix Raising avoided spam by changing structure at runtime.
Phoenix Omega avoids spam by pre-authoring many fixed, validated structures and choosing between them.
Same goal. Different enforcement point.

What “Teacher Mode” Means in Practice
In teacher mode, the book is about the teacher’s ideas and practices, presented by the author as a curator/interpreter of those teachings.

“Here are the ideas and exercises taught by [Teacher], adapted for you.”
Not:
a personal memoir
a generic self-help book
a loose inspiration collage

How Teacher Mode Changes the Book (System-Level)

How it works:

“In the writings of [Teacher]…”
“[Teacher] teaches that…”
Stories are still persona-adapted, but the source of truth is the teacher, not the author’s imagination.
System effect:
StoryInjectorV4 filters to teacher_mode=true

2. More exercises, clearly teacher-owned

System effect:
